<start> g . <end>
<start> justice . <end>
<start> The essential fact is : the greatest men may also perhaps have great virtues , but then they also have the opposites of these virtues . <end>
<start> I believe that it is precisely out of the presence of these opposites and of the feelings they resuscitate , that the great man arises , for the great man is the broad arch which spans two banks lying far apart . <end>
<start> In great men we find the specific qualities ol life in their highest manifestation : injustice , falsehood , exploitation . <end>
<start> But inasmuch as their effect has always been overwhelming , their essential nature has been most thoroughly misunderstood , M and interpreted as goodness . <end>
<start> The type of such an interpreter would be Carlyle . <end>
<start> Generally speaking , everything is worth no more and no less than one has paid for it . <end>
<start> This of course does not hold good in the case of an isolated individual : the great capacities of the individual have no relation whatsoever to that which he has done , sacrificed , and suffered for them . <end>
<start> But if one should examine the previous history of his race one would be sure to find the record of an extraordinary storing up and capitalising of power by means of all kinds of abstinence , struggle , industry , and determination . <end>
<start> It is because the great man has cost so much , and not because he stands there as a miracle , as a gift from heaven , or as an accident , that he became great : Heredity is a false notion . <end>
<start> A man s ancestors have always paid the price of what he is . <end>
<start> The danger of modesty . <end>
<start> To adapt ourselves too early to duties , societies , and daily schemes of work in which accident may have placed us , at a time when neither our powers nor our aim in life has stepped peremptorily into our consciousness This not only refers to Heroes and Hero Worship , but doubtless to Carlyle s prodigious misunderstanding of Goethe a misunderstanding which still requires to be put right by a critic untainted by Puritanism . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> the premature certainty of conscience and feeling of relief and of sociability which is acquired by this precocious , modest attitude , and which appears to our minds as a deliverance from those inner and outer disturbances of our feelings all this pampers and keeps a man down in the most dangerous fashion imaginable . <end>
<start> To learn to respect things which people about us respect , as if we had no standard or right of our own to determine values the strain of appraising things as others appraise them , counter to the whisperings of our inner taste , which also has a conscience of its own , becomes a terribly subtle kind of constraint : and if in the end no explosion takes place which bursts all the bonds of love and morality at once , then such a spirit becomes withered , dwarfed , feminine , and objective . <end>
<start> The reverse of this is bad enough , but still it is better than the foregoing : to suffer from one s environment , from its praise just as much as from its blame to be wounded by it and to fester inwardly without betraying the fact to defend one s self involuntarily and suspiciously against its love to learn to be silent , and perchance to conceal this by talking to create nooks and safe , lonely hiding places where one can go and take breath for a moment , or shed tears of sublime comfort until at last one has grown strong enough to say : What on earth have I to do with you ? <end>
<start> and to go one s way alone . <end>
<start> Those men who are in themselves destinies , and whose advent is the advent of fate , the whole race of heroic bearers of burdens : oh ! <end>
<start> how heartily and gladly would they have respite from themselves for once in a while ! <end>
<start> how they crave after stout hearts and shoulders , that they might free themselves , were it but for an hour or two , from that which oppresses them ! <end>
<start> And how fruitlessly they crave ! <end>
<start> They wait the obe rve all that passes before th er ey es : no man even cometh nigh to them with a tr ousancTth pan their suffering and pass ion no to wh at end they have waite d . <end>
<start> TTT At last , at last , they learn the first lesson of their life : to wait no longer and forthwith they learn their second lesson : to be affable , to be modest and from that time onwards to endure everybody and every kind of thing in short , to endure still a little more than they had endured heretofore . <end>
<start> The Highest Man as Lawgiver of the Future . <end>
<start> The lawgiver of the future . <end>
<start> After having tried for a long time in vain to attach a particular meaning to the word philosopher , for I found many antagonistic traits , I recognised that we can distinguish between two kinds of philosophers : Those who desire to establish any large system of values logical or moral Those who are the lawgiver of such value , tons . <end>
<start> The former try to seize upon the world of the present or the past , by embodying or abbreviating the multifarious phenomena by means of signs : their object is to make it possible for us to survey , to reflect upon , to comprehend , and to utilise everything that has happened hitherto they serve the purpose of man by using all past things to the benefit of his future . <end>
<start> The second class , however , are commanders they say : Thus shall it be ! <end>
<start> They alone determine the whither and the wherefore , and that which will be useful and beneficial to man they have command over the previous work of scientific men , and all knowledge is to them only a means to their creations . <end>
<start> This second kind of philosopher seldom appears and as a matter of fact their situation and their danger is appalling . <end>
<start> How often have they not intentionally blindfolded their eyes in order to shut out the sight of the small strip of ground which separates them from the abyss and from utter destruction . <end>
<start> Plato , for instance , when he persuaded himself that the good , as he wanted it , was not Plato s good , but the good in itself , the eternal treasure which a certain man of the name of Plato had chanced to find on his way ! <end>
<start> This same will to blindness prevails in a much coarser form in the case of the founders of religion their Thou shalt must on no account sound to their ears like I will , they only dare to pursue their task as if under the command of God their legislation of values can only be a burden they can bear if they regard it as revelation , in this way their conscience is not crushed by the responsibility . <end>
<start> that of Plato and that of Muhammed have been overthrown , and no thinker can any longer relieve his conscience with the hypothesis God or eternal values , the claim of the lawgiver to determine new values rises to an awfulness which has not yet been experienced . <end>
<start> Now those elect , on whom the faint light of such a duty is beginning to dawn , try and see whether they cannon escape it as their greatest danger by means of a timely side spring : for instance , they try to persuade themselves that their task is already accomplished , or that it defies accomplishment , or that their shoulders are not broad enough for such burdens , or that they are already taken up with burdens closer to hand , or even that this new and remote duty is a temptation and a seduction , drawing them away from all other duties a disease , a kind of madness . <end>
<start> Many , as a matter of fact , do succeed in evading the path appointed to them : throughout the whole of history we can see the traces of such deserters and their guilty consciences . <end>
<start> In most cases , however , there comes to such men of destiny that hour of delivery , that autumnal season of maturity , in which they are forced to do that which they did not even wish to do : and that deed before ! <end>
<start> which in the past they have trembled most , falls easily and sought from the tree , as an involve j try deed , almost as a present The human horizon . <end>
<start> Philosophers may be conceived as men who make the greatest efforts to discover to what extent man can elevate himself this holds good more particularly of Plato : how far maris power can extend . <end>
<start> But they do this as individuals perhaps the instinct of Caesars and of all founders of states , etc . <end>
<start> was greater , for it preoccupied itself with the question how far man could be urged forward in development under favourable circumstances . <end>
<start> What they did not sufficiently understand , however , was the nature of favourable circumstances . <end>
<start> The great question : Where has the plant man grown most magnificently heretofore ? <end>
<start> In order to answer this , a comparative study of history is necessary . <end>
<start> Every fact and every work exercises a fresh f j persuasion over every age and every new species of man . <end>
<start> History always enunciates new truths . <end>
<start> To remain objective , severe , firm , and hard while making a thought prevail is perhaps the best forte of artists but if for this purpose any one have to work upon human material as teachers , statesmen , have to do , etc . <end>
<start> then the repose , the coldness , and the hardness soon vanish . <end>
<start> In natures like Caesar and Napoleon we are able to divine something of the nature of disinterestedness in their work on their marble , whatever be the number of men that are sacrificed in the process . <end>
<start> In this direction the future of higher men lies : to bear the greatest responsibilities and not to go to rack and ruin through them . <end>
<start> Hitherto the deceptions of inspiration have almost always been necessary for a man not to lose faith in his own hand , and in his right to his task . <end>
<start> The reason why philosophers are mostly failures . <end>
<start> Because among the conditions which determine them there are qualities which generally ruin other men : A philosopher must have an enormous multiplicity of qualities he must be a sort of abbreviation of man and have all man s high and base desires : the danger of the contrast within him , and of the possibility of his loathing himself He must be inquisitive in an extraordinary number of ways : the danger of versatility He must be just and honest in the highest sense , but profound both in love and hate and in injustice He must not only be a spectator but a lawgiver : a judge and defendant in so far as he is an abbreviation of the world He must be extremely multiform and yet firm and hard . <end>
<start> He must be supple . <end>
<start> The really regal calling of the philosopher according to the expression of Alcuin the anglo-saxon : pravda , et rectal corroborate et santa sublimate . <end>
<start> V The new philosopher can only arise in conjure tion with a ruling class , as the highest of the latter . <end>
<start> Great politics , the rule of the earth , as a proximate contingency the total lack of principles necessary thereto . <end>
<start> Fundamental concept : the new values must first be created this remains our duty ! <end>
<start> The philosopher must be our lawgiver . <end>
<start> New species . <end>
<start> How the greatest species hitherto for instance , the Greeks were reared : this kind of accident must now be consciously striven for . <end>
<start> Nj Supposing one thinks of the philosopher as an educator who , looking down from his lonely elevation , is powerful enough to draw long chains of generations up to him : then he must be granted the most terrible privileges of a great educator . <end>
<start> An educator never says what he himself thinks but only that which he thinks it is good for those whom he is educating to hear upon any subject . <end>
<start> This dissimulation on his part must not be found out it is part of his masterless that people should believe in his honesty , he must be capable of all the means of discipline and education : there are some natures which he will only be able to raise by means of lashing them with his scorn others who are lazy , irresolute , cowardly , and vain , he will be able to affect only with exaggerated praise . <end>
<start> Such a teacher stands beyond good and evil , but nobody must know that he does . <end>
<start> We must not make men better , we must not talk to them about morality in any form as if j morality in itself , or an ideal kind of man in j general , could be taken for granted but we must create circumstances in which stronger men are j necessary , such as for their part will require a morality or , better still : a bodily and spiritual discipline which makes men strong , and upon which they will consequently insist ! <end>
<start> As they will need one so badly , they will have it . <end>
<start> We must not let ourselves be seduced by blue eyes and heaving breasts : greatness of soul has absolutely nothing romantic about it . <end>
<start> And unfortunately nothing whatever amiable either . <end>
<start> From warriors we must learn : to associate death with those interests for which we are fighting that makes us venerable we must learn to sacrifice numbers , and to take our cause sufficiently seriously not to spare men we must practise inexorable discipline , and allow ourselves violence and cunning in war . <end>
<start> The education which rears those ruling virtues that allow a man to become master of his geneva hence and his pity : the great disciplinary virtues Forgive thine enemies is mere child s play beside them , and the passions of the creator , must be elevated to the heights we must cease from carving marble ! <end>

<start> The more lofty philosophical man who is surrounded by loneliness , not because he wishes to be alone , but because he is what he is , and cannon find his equal : what a number of dangers and torments are reserved for him , precisely at the present time , when we have lost our belief in the order of rank , and consequently no longer know how to understand or honour this isolation ! <end>
<start> Formerly the sage almost sanctified himself in the consciences of the mob by going aside in this way to day the anchorage sees himself as though enveloped in a cloud of gloomy doubt and suspicions . <end>

<start> For men of such incomprehensible loneliness it is necessary to put a good stretch ot country between them and the of their fellows : this is part of their prudence . <end>

<start> Every attempt he makes to order his life in the present and with the present , every time he draws near to these men and their modern desires , he will have to expiate as if it were an actual sin : and withal he may look with wonder at the concealed wisdom of his nature , which after every one of these attempts immediately leads him back to himself by means of illnesses and painful accidents . <end>

<start> The most difficult and the highest form which man can attain is the most seldom successful thus the history of philosophy reveals a superabundance of bungled and unhappy cases of manhood , and its march is an extremely slow one whole centuries intervene and suppress what has been achieved : and in this way the is always made to fail . <end>

<start> From the days of my childhood I have reflected upon the sage s conditions of existence , and I will not conceal my happy conviction that in Europe he has once more become possible perhaps only for a short time . <end>
<start> These new philosophers begin with a description of a systematic order of rank and difference of value among men , what they desire is , alas precisely the reverse of an assimilation and equalisation of man : they teach estrangement in every sense , they cleave gulfs such as have never yet existed , and they would fain have man become more evil than he ever was . <end>

<start> How do men attain to great power and to great tasks ? <end>
<start> All the virtues and proficiency of the body and the soul are little by little laboriously acquired , through great industry , self control , and keeping one s self within narrow bounds , through a frequent , energetic , and genuine repetition of the same work and of the same hardships but there are men who are the heirs and masters of this slowly acquired and manifold treasure of virtues and proficiency because , owing to happy and reasonable marriages and also to lucky accidents , the acquired and accumulated forces of many generations , instead of being squandered and subdivided , have been assembled together by means of steadfast struggling and willing . <end>
<start> And thus , in the end , a man appears who is such a monster of strength , that he craves for a monstrous task . <end>

<start> DIONYSUS . <end>
<start> To him who is one of Nature s lucky strokes , to him unto whom my heart goes out , to him who is carved from one integral block , which is hard , sweet , and fragrant to him from whom even my nose can derive some pleasure let this book be dedicated . <end>
<start> His pleasure in anything ceases when the limits of what is beneficial to him are overstepped . <end>
<start> He divines the remedies for partial injuries his illnesses are the great stimulants of his existence . <end>
<start> He understands how to exploit his serious accidents . <end>
<start> He grows stronger under the misfortunes which threaten to annihilate him . <end>
<start> He instinctively gathers from all he sees , hears , and experiences , the materials for what concerns him most , he pursues a selective principle , he rejects a good deal . <end>
<start> and deliberate pride have bred in him , he tests the stimulus : whence does it come ? <end>
<start> whither does it lead ? <end>
<start> He does not submit . <end>
<start> He is always in his own company , whether his intercourse be with books , with men , or with Nature . <end>
<start> He honours anything by choosing it , by conceding to it , by trusting it . <end>
<start> We should attain to such a height , to such a lofty eagle s ledge , in our observation , as to be able to understand that everything happens , just as it ought to happen : and that all imperfection , and the pain it brings , belong to all that which is most eminently desirable . <end>
<start> Towards I experienced a fright for I saw that everything I had most wished for up to that time was being compromised . <end>
<start> I realised this when I perceived what Wagner was actually driving at : and I was bound very fast to him by all the bonds of a profound similarity of needs , by gratitude , by the thought that he could not be replaced , and by the absolute void which I saw facing me . <end>
<start> Just about this time I believed myself to be inextricably entangled in my . <end>
<start> philology y and my professorship in the accident and last shift of my life : I did not know how to get out of it , and was tired , used up , and on my last legs . <end>
<start> J At about the same time I realised that what my instincts most desired to attain was precisely the I reverse of what Schopenhauer s instincts wanted j that is to say , a justification of life , even where J it was most terrible , most equivocal , and most false : to this end , I had the formula Dionysian in my hand . <end>
<start> Schopenhauer s interpretation of the absolute as will was certainly a step towards that concept of the absolute which supposed it to be necessarily good , blessed , true , and integral but Schopenhauer did not understand how to defy this will : he remained suspended in the moralChristian ideal . <end>
<start> Indeed , he was still so very much under the dominion of Christian values , that , once he could no longer regard the absolute as God , he had to conceive it as evil , foolish , utterly reprehensible . <end>
<start> He did not realise that there is an infinite number of ways of being different , and even of being God . <end>
<start> Hitherto , moral values have been the highest values : does anybody doubt this ? <end>
<start> If we bring down the values from their pedestal , we thereby alter all values : the principle of their order of rank which has prevailed hitherto is thus overthrown . <end>
<start> Transvalue values what does this mean ? <end>
<start> It implies that all spontaneous motives , all new , future , and stronger motives , are still extant but that they now appear under false names and false valuations , and have not yet become conscious of themselves . <end>
<start> We ought to have the courage to become , conscious , and to affirm all that which has been attained to get rid of the humdrum character of old valuations , which makes us unworthy of the best and strongest things that we have achieved . <end>
<start> Any doctrine would be superfluous for which everything is not already prepared in the way of accumulated forces and explosive material . <end>
<start> A of values can only be accomplished when there is a tension of new needs , and a new set of needy people who feel all old values as painful , although they are not conscious of what is wrong . <end>
<start> The standpoint from which my values are determined : is abundance or desire active ? <end>
<start> Is one a mere spectator , or is one s own shoulder at the wheel is one looking away or is one turning aside ? <end>
<start> Is one acting spontaneously , as the j result of accumulated strength , or is one merely reacting to a goad or to a stimulus ? <end>
<start> Is one simply acting as the result of a paucity of elements , or of such an overwhelming dominion over a host of elements that this power enlists the latter into its service if it requires them ? <end>
<start> Is one al Is one perfect through the smallness of the task , or imperfect owing to the extraordinary character of the aim ? <end>
<start> Is one genuine or only an actor ian actor ? <end>
<start> is one a representative or the of personalities ? <end>
<start> Is one ill from aa shepherd , or as an exception third alternative : one play the clown ? <end>
<start> Is one in search of resistance , one s precocity or to one s tardiness ? <end>
<start> Is it one of garish parts ? <end>
<start> Is one proud enough not to a bite of conscience this species is becoming as if it now no longer had enough teeth to do ! <end>
<start> are some people who would lose the whole joy of their lives if they were deprived of their duty thistle born subjects . <end>
<start> Supposing our common comprehension of the universe were a misunderstandings would it be possible to conceive of a form of perfection , within the limits of which even such a misunderstanding as this could be sanctioned ? <end>
<start> which does not correspond to our logic , to our u beauty , to our good , to our truth , might be perfect in a higher sense even than our ideal is . <end>
<start> Our most important limitation : we must not defy the unknown we are just beginning to know so little . <end>
<start> The false and wasted endeavours . <end>
<start> Our new world : we must ascertain to what extent we are the creators of our valuations we will thus be able to put sense into history . <end>
<start> This belief in truth is reaching its final logical conclusion in us ye know how it reads : that if there is anything at all that must be worshipped it is appearance that falsehood and not truth is divine . <end>
<start> He who urges rational thought forward , thereby also drives its antagonistic power mysticism and cookery of every kind to new feats of strength . <end>
<start> We should recognise that every movement is i partly the manifestation of fatigue resulting from a previous movement satiety after it , the malice of weakness towards it , and disease and partly a newly awakened accumulation of long slumbering forces , and therefore wanton , violent , healthy . <end>
<start> Health and : let us be careful ! <end>
<start> The standard is the bloom of the body , the agility , courage , and cheerfulness of the mind but also , of s course , how much a man can bear and overcome , and convert into health . <end>
<start> That which would send more delicate natures to the dogs , belongs to the stimulating means of great health . <end>
<start> It is only a question of power : to have all the i morbid traits of the century , but to balance them by means of overflowing , plastic , and rejuvenating I power . <end>
<start> The strong man . <end>
<start> Concerning the strength of the nineteenth century . <end>
<start> We are more mediaeval than the eighteenth century not only more inquisitive or more susceptible to the strange and to the rare . <end>
<start> We have revolted against the Revolution . <end>
<start> We have freed ourselves from the fear of reason , which was the spectre of the eighteenth century : we once more dare to be childish , lyrical , absurd , in a word , we are musicians . <end>
<start> And we are just as little frightened of the ridiculous as of the absurd . <end>
<start> The devil finds that he is tolerated even by God : better still , he has become interesting as one who has been misunderstood and slandered for ages , we are the saviours of the devil s honour . <end>
<start> We no longer separate from the terrible . <end>
<start> We reconcile good things , in all their complexity , This is reminiscent of Goethe s Faust . <end>
<start> See Prologue in Heaven . <end>
<start> Tr . <end>
<start> with the very worst things we have overcome the desideratum of the pas which wanted goodness to grow without the increase of evil . <end>
<start> The cowardice towards the ideal , peculiar to the Renaissance , has ! <end>
<start> diminished we even dare to aspire to the latter morality . <end>
<start> Intolerance towards priests and the Church has at the same time come to an end It is immoral to believe in God but this is precisely what we regard as the best possible justification of this belief . <end>
<start> On all these things we have conferred the civic rights of our minds . <end>
<start> We do not tremble before the back side of good things we even look for it , we are brave and inquisitive enough for that , of Greek antiquity , of morality , of reason , of good taste , for instance we reckon up the losses which we incur with all this treasure : we almost reduce ourselves to poverty with such a treasure . <end>
<start> Neither do we conceal the back side of evil things from ourselves . <end>
<start> That which does us honour . <end>
<start> If anything does us honour , it is this : we have transferred our seriousness to other things all those things which have been despised and laid aside as base by all ages , we regard as important on the other hand , we surrender fine feelings at a cheap rate . <end>
<start> Could any aberration be more dangerous than the contempt of the body ? <end>
<start> As if all intellectuality were not thereby condemned to become morbid , and to take refuge in the vapours of idealism ! <end>
<start> and idealists holds water : we are more radical . <end>
<start> We have discovered the smallest world everywhere as the most decisive . <end>
<start> The paving stones in the streets , good air in our rooms , food understood according to its worth : we value all the necessaries of life seriously , and despise all beautiful as a form of levity and frivolity . <end>
<start> That which has been most despised hitherto , is now pressed into the front rank . <end>
<start> In the place of Rousseau s man of Nature , the nineteenth century has discovered a much more genuine image of Man , it had the courage to do this . <end>
<start> On the whole , the Christian concept of man has in a way been installed . <end>
<start> What we have not had the courage to do , was to call precisely this man par excellence good , and to see the future of mankind guaranteed in him . <end>
<start> In the same way , we did not dare to regard the growth in the terrible side of man s character as an accompanying feature of every advance in culture in this sense we are still under the influence of the Christian ideal , and side with it against paganism , and likewise against the Renaissance concept of virtue . <end>
<start> But the key of culture is not to be found in this way : and in praxis we still have the forgeries of history in favour of the good man as if he alone constituted the progress of humanity and the socialistic ideal i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> the residue of Christianity and of Rousseau in the deChristianised world . <end>
<start> The fight against the eighteenth century : it meets with its greatest conquerors in Goethe and Napoleon . <end>
<start> Schopenhauer , too , rights against the eighteenth century but he returns involuntarily to the seventeenth he is a modern Pascal , with Pascalianj valuations , without Christianity . <end>
<start> Schopenhauer wai not strong enough to invent a new yea . <end>
<start> Napoleon : we see the necessary relationship between the higher and the terrible man . <end>
<start> Man installed , and her due of contempt and fear restored to woman . <end>
<start> Highest activity and health are the signs of the great man the straight line and grand style rediscovered in action the mightiest of all instincts , that of life itself , the lust of dominion , heartily welcomed Revue des deux modes , th February . <end>
<start> maine concerning Napoleon Suddenly the master faculty reveals itself : the artist , which was latent in the politician , comes forth from his scabbard he creates dans ideal et l impossible . <end>
<start> He is once more recognised as that which he is : the posthumous brother of Dante and of Michelangelo and verily , in view of the definite contours of his vision , the intensity , the coherence , and inner consistency of his dream , the depth of his meditations , the superhuman greatness of his conception , he is their equal : son genie a la mime taille et la mime structure il est un des trois esprit de la renaissance . <end>
<start> internal economy of the primitive ve man s soul , the fear of evil . <end>
<start> What is evil Three kinds of things : accident , uncertainty , the unexpected . <end>
<start> How does primitive man combat evil ? <end>
<start> He conceives it as a thing of reason , of power , even as a person . <end>
<start> By this means he is enabled to make treaties with it , and generally to operate upon it in advance to forestall it . <end>
<start> Another expedient is to declare its evil and harmful character to be but apparent : the consequences of accidental occurrences , and of uncertainty and the unexpected , are interpreted as well-meant , as reasonable . <end>
<start> A third means is to interpret evil , above all , as merited : evil is thus justified as a punishment . <end>
<start> In short , man submits to it : all religious and moral interpretations are but forms of submission to evil . <end>
<start> The belief that a good purpose lies behind all evil , implies the renunciation of any desire to combat it . <end>
<start> Now , the history of every culture shows a diminution of this fear of the accidental , of the uncertain , and of the unexpected . <end>
<start> Culture means precisely , to learn to reckon , to discover causes , to acquire the power of forestalling events , to acquire a belief in necessity . <end>
<start> With the growth of culture , man is able to dispense with that primitive form of submission to evil called religion or morality , and that justification of evil . <end>
<start> Now he wages war against evil , he gets rid of it . <end>
<start> Yes , a state of security , of belief in law and the possibility of calculation , is possible , in which consciousness regards these things with tedium , in which the joy of the accidental , of the uncertain , and of the unexpected , actually becomes a spur . <end>
<start> Let us halt a moment before this symptom of highest culture , I call it the pessimism of strength . <end>
<start> Man now no longer requires a justification of evil justification is precisely what he abhors : he enjoys evil , pur , cm he regards purposeless evil as the most interesting kind of evil . <end>
<start> If he had required a God in the past , he now delights in cosmic disorder without a God , a world of accident , to the essence of which terror , ambiguity , and seductiveness belong . <end>
<start> In a state of this sort , it is precisely goodness which requires to be justified that is to say , it must either have an evil and a dangerous basis , or else it must contain a vast amount of stupidity : in which case it still pleases . <end>
<start> Animality no longer awakens terror now a very intellectual and happy wanton spirit in favour of the animal in man , is , in such periods , the most triumphant form of spirituality . <end>
<start> man now strong enough to be able to feel ashamed of a belief in God , he may now play the part of the devil s advocate afresh . <end>
<start> If in practice he pretends to uphold virtue , it will be for those reasons which lead virtue to be associated with subtlety , cunning , lust of gain , and a form of the lust of power . <end>
<start> This pessimism of strength also ends in a theodicy , i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> in an absolute saying of yea to the world but the same arguments will be raised in favour of life which formerly were raised against it : and in this way , in a conception of this world as the highest ideal possible , which has been effectively attained . <end>
<start> inthe pessimism of sensitiveness excessive irritability with a preponderance of the feelings of pain . <end>
<start> The pessimism of the will that is not free otherwise expressed : the lack of resisting power against stimuli . <end>
<start> The pessimism of doubt shyness in regard to everything fixed , in regard to all grasping and touching . <end>
<start> The psychological conditions which belong to these different kinds of pessimism , may all be observed in a lunatic asylum , even though they are there found in a slightly exaggerated form . <end>
<start> The same applies to Nihilism the penetrating feeling of nonentity . <end>
<start> What , however , is the nature of Pascal s moral pessimism , and the metaphysical pessimism of the veranda Philosophy ? <end>
<start> What is the nature of the social pessimism of anarchists as of Shelley , and of the pessimism of compassion like that of Leo Tolstoy and of Alfred de vinny ? <end>
<start> Are all these things not also the phenomena of decay and sickness ? <end>
<start> And is not excessive seriousness in regard to moral values , or in regard to other world fictions , or social calamities , or suffering in general , of the same order ? <end>
<start> All such exaggeration of a single and narrow standpoint is in itself a sign of sickness . <end>
<start> The same applies to the preponderance of a negative over an affirmative attitude ! <end>
<start> In this respect we must not confound with the above : the joy of saying and doing no , which is the result of the enormous power and tenseness of an affirmative attitude peculiar to all rich and mighty men and ages . <end>
<start> It is , as it were , a luxury , a form of courage too , which opposes the terrible , which has sympathy with the frightful and the questionable because , among other things , one is terrible and questionable : the Dionysian in will , intellect , and taste . <end>
<start> My fight against the feeling of sin and the introduction of the notion of punishment into the physical and metaphysical world , likewise into psychology and the interpretation of history . <end>
<start> The I recognition of the fact that all philosophies and valuations hitherto have been saturated with morality . <end>
<start> My identification and my discovery of the traditional ideal , of the Christian ideal , even where the dogmatic form of Christianity has been wrecked . <end>
<start> The danger of tie Christian ideal resides in its valuations , in that which can dispense with concrete expression : my struggle against latent Christianity for instance , in music , in Socialism . <end>
<start> My struggle against the eighteenth century of Rousseau , against his Nature , against his goodman , his belief in the dominion of feeling against the pampering , weakening , and moralising of man : an ideal born of the hatred of aristocratic culture , which in practice is the dominion of unbridled feelings of resentment , and invented as a standard for the purpose of war the Christian morality of the feeling of sin , as well as the morality of resentment , is an attitude of the mob . <end>
<start> My fight against Romanticism , in which the ideals of Christianity and of Rousseau converge , but which possesses at the same time a yearning for that antiquity which knew of sacerdotal and aristocratic culture , a yearning for virtue , and for the strong man something extremely hybrid a false and imitated kind of stronger humanity , which appreciates extreme conditions in general and sees the symptom of strength in them the cult of passion an imitation of the most expressive forms , furore expressive , originating not out of plenitude , but out of want . <end>
<start> In the nineteenth century there are some things which are born out of relative plenitude i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> out of well being , cheerful music , etc . <end>
<start> among poets , for instance , stiffer and Gottfried Keller give signs of more strength and inner well being than . <end>
<start> The great strides of engineering , of inventions , of the natural sciences and of history ? <end>
<start> are relative products of the strength and self reliance of the nineteenth century . <end>
<start> My struggle against the predominance of gregarious instincts , now science makes common cause with them against the profound hate with which every kind of order of rank and of aloofness . <end>
<start> From the pressure of plenitude , from the tension of forces that are continually increasing within us and which cannon yet discharge themselves , a condition is produced which is very similar to that which precedes a storm : we like Nature s sky become overcast . <end>
<start> That , too , is pessimism . <end>
<start> A teaching which puts an end to such a condition by the fact that it commands something : a of values by means of which the accumulated forces are given a channel , a direction , so that they explode into deeds and flashes of lightning does not in the least require to be a hedonistic teaching : in so far as it releases strength which was compressed to an agonising degree , it brings happiness . <end>
<start> Pleasure appears with the feeling of power . <end>
<start> Happiness means that the consciousness of power and triumph has begun to prevail . <end>
<start> Progress is the strengthening of the type , the ability to exercise great will power : everything else is a misunderstanding and a danger . <end>
<start> There comes a time when the old masquerade and moral logging up of the passions provokes repugnance : naked Nature when the quanta of power are recognised as decidedly simple as determining rank when grand style appears again as the result of great passion . <end>
<start> V The purpose of culture would have us enlist everything terrible , step by step and experimentally , into its service but before it is strong enough for this it must combat , moderate , mask , and even curse everything terrible . <end>
<start> Wherever a culture points to anything as evil , it betrays its fear and therefore weakness . <end>
<start> Thesis : everything good is the evil of yore which has been rendered serviceable . <end>
<start> Standard : the more terrible and the greater the passions may be which an age , a people , and an individual are at liberty to possess , because they are able to use them as a means y the higher is their culture , the more mediocre , weak , submissive , and cowardly a man may be , the more things he will regard as evil : according to him the kingdom of evil is the largest . <end>
<start> The lowest man will see the kingdom of evil i . <end>
<start> e . <end>
<start> that which is forbidden him and which is hostile to him everywhere . <end>
<start> It is not a fact that happiness follows virtue but it is the mighty man who first declares his happy state to be virtue , f Evil actions belong to the mighty and the virtuous : bad and base actions belong to the subjected . <end>

<start> The perishable nature of existence might be interpreted as the joy of procreative and destructive force , as unremitting creation . <end>
<start> The word Dionysian expresses : a constraint to unity , a soaring above personality , the common place , society , reality , and above the abyss of the ephemeral the passionately painful sensation of superabundance , in darker , fuller , and more fluctuating conditions an ecstatic saying of yea to the collective character of existence , as that which remains the same , and equally mighty and blissful throughout all change the great pantheistic sympathy with pleasure and pain , which declares even the most terrible and most questionable qualities of existence good , and sanctified them the eternal will to procreation , to fruitfulness , and to recurrence the feeling of unity in regard to the necessity of creating and annihilating . <end>
<start> The word apollonius expresses : the constraint to be absolutely isolated , to the typical individual , to everything that simplifies , distinguishes , and makes strong , salient , definite , and typical : to freedom within the law . <end>

<start> It is clear that only the rarest and most lucky cases of humanity can attain to the highest and most sublime human joys in which Life celebrates its own glorification and this only happens when these rare creatures themselves and their forbears have lived a long preparatory life leading to this goal , without , however , having done so consciously . <end>

<start> From that height of joy in which man feels himself completely and utterly a deified form and self-justification of nature , down to the joy of healthy peasants and healthy semi human beasts , the whole of this long and enormous gradation of the light and colour of happiness was called by the Greek not without that grateful quivering of one who is initiated into secret , not without much caution and pious silence by the godlike name : Dionysus . <end>
<start> What then do all modern men the children of a crumbling , multifarious , sick and strange age know of the compass of Greek happiness , how could they know anything about it ! <end>

<start> Dionysus is a judge ! <end>
<start> Am I understood ? <end>
<start> There can be no doubt that the Greeks sought to interpret , by means of their Dionysian experiences , the final mysteries of the destiny of the soul and everything they knew concerning the education and the purification of man , and above all concerning the absolute hierarchy and inequality of value between man and man . <end>
<start> There is the deepest experience of all Greeks , which they conceal beneath great silence , we do not know the Greeks so long as this hidden and subterranean access to them remains obstructed . <end>
<start> The indiscreet eyes of scholars will never perceive anything in these things , however much learned energy may still have to be expended in the service of this excavation even the noble zeal of such friends of antiquity as Goethe and Winckelmann , seems to savour somewhat of bad form and of arrogance , precisely in this respect . <end>
<start> To wait and to prepare oneself to await the appearance of new sources of knowledge to prepare oneself in solitude for the sight of new faces and the sound of new voices to cleanse one s soul ever more and more of the dust and noise , as of a country fair , which is peculiar to this age to overcome everything Christian by something super Christian , and not only to rid oneself of it , for the Christian doctrine is the to the Dionysian to rediscover the South in oneself , and to stretch a clear , glittering , and mysterious southern sky above one to reconquer the southern healthiness and concealed power of the soul , once more for oneself to increase the compass of one s soul step by step , and to become more supranational , more European , more superEuropean , more Oriental , and finally more Hellenic for Hellenism was , as a matter of fact , the first great union and synthesis of everything Oriental , and precisely on that account , the beginning of the European soul , the discovery of our new world : he who lives under such imperatives , who knows what he may not encounter some day ? <end>

<start> The two types : Dionysus and Christ on the Cross . <end>

<start> ETERNAL RECURRENCE . <end>
<start> My philosophy reveals the triumphant thought through which all other systems of thought must ultimately perish . <end>
<start> It is the great disciplinary thought : those races that cannon bear it are doomed those which regard it as the greatest blessing are destined to rule . <end>
<start> The greatest of all fights : for this purpose a new weapon is required . <end>
<start> A hammer : a terrible alternative must be created . <end>
<start> Europe must be brought face to face with the logic of facts , and confronted with the question whether its will for ruin is really earnest . <end>
<start> General levelling down to mediocrity must be avoided . <end>
<start> Rather than this it would be preferable to perish . <end>
<start> A pessimistic attitude of mind and a pessimistic doctrine and ecstatic Nihilism , may in certain circumstances even prove indispensable to the philosopher that is to say , as a mighty form of pressure , or hammer , with which he can smash up degenerate , perishing races and put them out of existence with which he can beat a track to a new order of life , or instil a longing for nonentity in those who are degenerate and who . <end>
<start> desire to perish . <end>

<start> decisive manner . <end>
<start> Everything becomes and returns for ever , escape is impossible ! <end>
<start> Granted that we could appraise the value of existence , what would be the result of it ? <end>
<start> The thought of recurrence is a principle of selection in the service of power and barbarity ! <end>
<start> The ripeness of man for this thought . <end>
<start> The thought of eternal recurrence : its first principles which must necessarily be true if it were true . <end>
<start> what its result is . <end>
<start> It is the most oppressive thought : its probable results , provided it be not prevented , that is to say , provided all values be not . <end>
<start> in certainty , but in uncertainty no longer cause and effect , but continual creativeness no longer the will to self preservation , but to power no longer the modest expression it is all only subjective , but it is all our work ! <end>
<start> let us be proud of it . <end>
<start> in order to endure the thought of recurrence , freedom from morality is necessary new means against the i oX pain pain regarded as the instrument , as the father of pleasure there is no secretive consciousness of pain pleasure derived from all kinds of uncertainty and tentativeness , as a counterpoise to extreme fatalism suppression of the concept necessity suppression of the will suppression of absolute knowledge . <end>
<start> Greatest elevation of man s consciousness of strength , as that which creates superman . <end>
<start> The two extremes of thought the materialistic j and the platonic are reconciled in eternal recurrence : both are regarded as ideals . <end>
<start> If the universe had a goal , that goal would h ave been reached by now . <end>
<start> i and ort oT unforeseen final state exist d , that state also wou ld have been reached . <end>
<start> If It were capable of any Halting or stability of any being , it would only have possessed this capability of becoming stable for one instant in its development and again becoming would have been at an end for ages , and with it all thinking and all spirit . <end>
<start> The fact of intellects being in a state of development proves that the universe can have no goa l , no final state , and is incapable of being . <end>
<start> But the old habit of thinking of some purpose in regard to all phenomena , and of thinking of a directing and creating deity in regard to the universe , is so powerful , that the thinker has to go to great pains in order to avoid thinking of the very aimlessness of the world as intended . <end>
<start> The idea that the universe intentionally evades a goal , and even knows artificial means herewith it prevents itself from falling into a circular movement , must occur to all those who would fain attribute to the universe the capacity of eternally regenerating itself that is to say , they would fain impose upon a finite , definite force which is invariable in quantity , like the universe , the miraculous gift of renewing its forms and its conditions for all eternity . <end>
<start> Although the universe is no longer a God , it must still be capable of the divine power of creating and transforming it must forbid itself to relapse into any one of its previous forms it must not only have the intention , but also the means , of avoiding any sort of repetition every second of its existence , even , it must control every single one of its movements , with the view of avoiding goals , final states , and . <end>
<start> repetitions and all the other results of such an unpardonable and insane method of thought and desire . <end>
<start> All this is nothing more than the old religious mode of thought and desire , which , in spite of all , longs to believe that in some way or other the universe resembles the old , beloved , infinite , and God that in some way or other the old God still lives that longing of Spinoza s which is expressed in the words deus give natura what he really felt was natura give deus . <end>
<start> Which , then , is the proposition and belief in which the decisive change , the present preponderance of the scientific spirit over the religious and spirit , is best formulated ? <end>
<start> Ought it not to be : the universe , as force , must not be thought of as unlimited , because it cannon be thought of in this way , we forbid ourselves the concept infinite force , because it is incompatible with the idea of force ? <end>
<start> Whence it follows that the universe lacks the power of eternal renewal . <end>
<start> The principle of the conservation of energy inevitably involves eternal recurrence . <end>
<start> that a state of equilibrium has never been reached , proves that it is impossible . <end>
<start> But in infinite space it must have been reached . <end>
<start> Likewise in spherical space . <end>
<start> The . <end>
<start> form of space must be the cause of the eternal movement , and ultimately of all imperfection . <end>
<start> That energy and stability and immutability are contradictory . <end>
<start> The measure of energy dimensional is fixed , though it is essentially fluid . <end>
<start> That which is timeless must be refuted . <end>
<start> At any given moment of energy , the absolute conditions for a new distribution of all forces are present it cannon remain stationary . <end>
<start> Change is part of its essence , therefore time is as well : by this means , however , the necessity of change has only been established once more in theory . <end>
<start> A certain emperor always bore the fleeting nature of all things in his mind , in order not to value them too seriously , and to be able to live quietly in their midst . <end>
<start> Conversely , everything seems to me much too important for it to be so fleeting I seek an eternity for everything : ought one to pour the most precious sales and wines into the sea ? <end>
<start> My consolation is that everything that has been is eternal : the sea will wash it up again . <end>
<start> The neil concept of the universe . <end>
<start> The universe exists it is nothing that grows into existence and that passes out of existence . <end>
<start> Or , better still , it develops , it passes away , but it never began to develop , and has never ceased from passing away it maintains itself in both states . <end>
<start> It lives on itself , its excrement are its nourishment . <end>
<start> We need not concern ourselves for one instant with the hypothesis of a created world . <end>
<start> The concept create is to day utterly indefinable and unrealisable it is but a word which hails from superstitious ages nothing can be explained with a word . <end>
<start> The last attempt that was made to conceive of a world that began occurred quite recently , in many cases with the help of logical reasoning , generally , too , as you will guess , with an ulterior theological motive . <end>
<start> Several attempts have been made lately to show that the concept that the universe has an infinite past regresses in is contradictory : it was even demonstrated , it is true , at the price of confounding the head with the tail . <end>
<start> Nothing can prevent me from calculating backwards from this moment of time , and of saying : I shall never reach the end just as I can calculate without end in a forward direction , from the same moment . <end>
<start> It is only when I wish to commit the error I shall be careful to avoid it of reconcile ing this correct concept of a regresses in infinite with the absolutely unrealisable concept of a finite progresses up to the present only when I consider the direction forwards or backwards as logically indifferent , that I take hold of the head j this very moment and think I hold the tail : this pleasure I leave to you , Mr . <end>
<start> dithering ! <end>
<start> j I have come across this thought in other thinkers before me , and every time I found that it was determined by other ulterior motives chiefly theological , in favour of a creator spirits . <end>
<start> If the universe were in any way able to congeal , to dry up , to perish or if it were capable of attaining to a state of equilibrium or if it had any kind of goal at all which a long lapse of time , immutability , and finality reserved for it in short , to speak metaphysically , if becoming could resolve itself into being or into nonentity , this state ought already to have been reached . <end>
<start> H But it has not been reached : it therefore follows . <end>
<start> This is the only certainty we can grasp , which can serve as a corrective to a host of cosmic hypotheses possible in themselves . <end>
<start> If , for instance , materialism cannon consistently escape the conclusion of a finite state , which William Thomson has traced out for it , then materialism is thereby refuted . <end>
<start> If the universe may be conceived as a definite quantity of energy , as a definite number of centres of energy , and every other concept remains indefinite and therefore useless , it follows therefrom that the universe must go through a calculable number of combinations in the great game of chance which constitutes its existence . <end>
<start> In infinity at some moment or other , every possible combine tion must once have been realised not only this , i but it must have been realised an infinite number of times . <end>
<start> And inasmuch as between every one of these combinations and its next recurrence I every other possible combination would necessarily have been undergone , and since every one of these combinations would determine the whole series in the same order , a circular movement of absolutely identical series is thus demonstrated : the universe is thus shown to be a circular movement which has already repeated itself an infinite number of times , and which plays its game for all eternity , This conception is not simply materialistic tor if it were this , it would not involve an infinite recurrence of identical cases , but a finite state . <end>
<start> Owing to the fact that the universe has not reached this finite state , materialism shows itself to be but an imperfect and provisional he do the sis . <end>
<start> And do ye know what the universe is to my mind ? <end>
<start> Shall i show it to you in my mirror ? <end>
<start> This universe is a monster of energy , without beginning or end a fixed and brazen quantity oi energy which grows neither bigger nor smaller , which does not consume itself , but only alters its face as a whole its bulk is immutable , it is a household without either losses or gains , but likewise without increase and without sources of revenue , surrounded by nonentity as by a frontier . <end>
<start> It is nothing vague or wasteful , it does not stretch into infinity but it is a definite quantum of energy located in limited space , and not in space which would be anywhere empty . <end>
<start> It is rather energy everywhere , the play of forces and force waves , at the same time one and many , agglomeration here and diminishing there , a sea of forces storming and raging in itself , for ever changing , for ever rolling back over incalculable ages to recurrence , with an ebb and flow of its forms , producing the most complicated things out of the most simple structures producing the most ardent , most savage , and not contradictory things out of the quietest , most rigid , and most frozen material , and then returning from to uniformity , from the play of contradictions back into the delight of consonance , saying yea unto itself , even in this homogeneity of its courses and ages for ever blessing itself as so letting which recurs for all eternity , a becoming which know s not satiety , or disgust , or weariness : this , my dionysian world of eternal self creation , of eternal self destruction , this mysterious world of twofold voluptuousness this , my Beyond Good and Evil without aim , unless there . <end>
<start> is an aim in the bliss of the circle , without will , unless a ring must by nature keep goodwill to itself , would you have a name for my world ? <end>
<start> A solution of all your riddles ? <end>
<start> Do ye also want a light , ye most concealed , strongest and most undaunted men of the blackest midnight ? <end>
<start> This world is the Will to Power and nothing else ! <end>
<start> And even ye yourselves are this will to power and nothing besides ! <end>
